    <title>Frontiers | De novo Assembly of the Pokeweed Genome Provides Insight Into Pokeweed Antiviral Protein (PAP) Gene Expression | Plant Science</title>

    <link rel="shortcut icon" href="https://3718aeafc638f96f5bd6-d4a9ca15fc46ba40e71f94dec0aad28c.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/favicon_16x16.ico" type="image/x-icon" />

    <meta property="og:title" name="Title" content="De novo Assembly of the Pokeweed Genome Provides Insight Into Pokeweed Antiviral Protein (PAP) Gene Expression" />
    <meta property="og:description" name="Description" content="Ribosome-inactivating proteins (RIPs) are RNA glycosidases thought to function in defense against pathogens. These enzymes remove purine bases from RNAs, including rRNA; the latter activity decreases protein synthesis in vitro, which is hypothesized to limit pathogen proliferation by causing host cell death. Pokeweed antiviral protein (PAP) is a RIP synthesized by the American pokeweed plant (Phytolacca americana). PAP inhibits virus infection when expressed in crop plants, yet little is known about the function of PAP in pokeweed due to a lack of genomic tools for this non-model species. In this work, we de novo assembled the pokeweed genome and annotated protein-coding genes. Sequencing comprised paired-end reads from a short-insert library of 83X coverage, and our draft assembly (N50 = 42.5 Kb) accounted for 74% of the measured pokeweed genome size of 1.3 Gb. We obtained 29,773 genes, 73% of which contained known protein domains, and identified several PAP isoforms. Within the gene models of each PAP isoform, a long 5′ UTR intron was discovered, which was validated by RT-PCR and sequencing. Presence of the intron stimulated reporter gene expression in tobacco. To gain further understanding of PAP regulation, we complemented this genomic resource with expression profiles of pokeweed plants subjected to stress treatments [jasmonic acid (JA), salicylic acid, polyethylene glycol, and wounding]. Cluster analysis of the top differentially expressed genes indicated that some P..." />

        <meta name="citation_journal_abbrev" content="Front. Plant Sci." />
        <meta name="citation_issn" content="1664-462X" />
        <meta name="citation_doi" content="10.3389/fpls.2019.01002" />
        <meta name="citation_pages" content="1002" />
        <meta name="citation_language" content="English" />
        <meta name="citation_title" content="De novo Assembly of the Pokeweed Genome Provides Insight Into Pokeweed Antiviral Protein (PAP) Gene Expression" />
        <meta name="citation_keywords" content="Cis regulatory elements; Jasmonic acid; Phytolacca americana; Pokeweed; Pokeweed antiviral protein (PAP); Ribosome inactivating protein (RIP); genome assembly" />
        <meta name="citation_abstract" content="Ribosome-inactivating proteins are RNA glycosidases thought to function in defense against pathogens. These enzymes remove purine bases from RNAs, including rRNA; the latter activity decreases protein synthesis in vitro, which is hypothesized to limit pathogen proliferation by causing host cell death. Pokeweed antiviral protein (PAP) is a ribosome-inactivating protein synthesized by the American pokeweed plant (Phytolacca americana). PAP inhibits virus infection when expressed in crop plants, yet little is known about the function of PAP in pokeweed due to a lack of genomic tools for this non-model species. In this work, we de novo assembled the pokeweed genome and annotated protein-coding genes. Sequencing comprised paired-end reads from a short-insert library of 83X coverage, and our draft assembly (N50 = 42.5 Kb) accounted for 74% of the measured pokeweed genome size of 1.3 Gb. We obtained 29,773 genes, 73% of which contained known protein domains, and identified several PAP isoforms. Within the gene models of each PAP isoform, a long 5’ UTR intron was discovered, which was validated by RT-PCR and sequencing. Presence of the intron stimulated reporter gene expression in tobacco. To gain further understanding of PAP regulation, we complemented this genomic resource with expression profiles of pokeweed plants subjected to stress treatments (jasmonic acid, salicylic acid, polyethylene glycol, and wounding). Cluster analysis of the top differentially expressed genes indicated that some PAP isoforms shared expression patterns with genes involved in terpenoid biosynthesis, jasmonic acid-mediated signalling, and metabolism of amino acids and carbohydrates. The newly sequenced promoters of all PAP isoforms contained cis-regulatory elements associated with diverse biotic and abiotic stresses. These elements mediated response to jasmonic acid in tobacco, based on reporter constructs containing promoter truncations of PAP-I, the most abundant isoform. Taken together, this first genomic resource for the Phytolaccaceae plant family provides new insight into the regulation and function of PAP in pokeweed." />
        <meta name="description" content="Ribosome-inactivating proteins are RNA glycosidases thought to function in defense against pathogens. These enzymes remove purine bases from RNAs, including rRNA; the latter activity decreases protein synthesis in vitro, which is hypothesized to limit pathogen proliferation by causing host cell death. Pokeweed antiviral protein (PAP) is a ribosome-inactivating protein synthesized by the American pokeweed plant (Phytolacca americana). PAP inhibits virus infection when expressed in crop plants, yet little is known about the function of PAP in pokeweed due to a lack of genomic tools for this non-model species. In this work, we de novo assembled the pokeweed genome and annotated protein-coding genes. Sequencing comprised paired-end reads from a short-insert library of 83X coverage, and our draft assembly (N50 = 42.5 Kb) accounted for 74% of the measured pokeweed genome size of 1.3 Gb. We obtained 29,773 genes, 73% of which contained known protein domains, and identified several PAP isoforms. Within the gene models of each PAP isoform, a long 5’ UTR intron was discovered, which was validated by RT-PCR and sequencing. Presence of the intron stimulated reporter gene expression in tobacco. To gain further understanding of PAP regulation, we complemented this genomic resource with expression profiles of pokeweed plants subjected to stress treatments (jasmonic acid, salicylic acid, polyethylene glycol, and wounding). Cluster analysis of the top differentially expressed genes indicated that some PAP isoforms shared expression patterns with genes involved in terpenoid biosynthesis, jasmonic acid-mediated signalling, and metabolism of amino acids and carbohydrates. The newly sequenced promoters of all PAP isoforms contained cis-regulatory elements associated with diverse biotic and abiotic stresses. These elements mediated response to jasmonic acid in tobacco, based on reporter constructs containing promoter truncations of PAP-I, the most abundant isoform. Taken together, this first genomic resource for the Phytolaccaceae plant family provides new insight into the regulation and function of PAP in pokeweed." />

            <div class="abstract-container">
                
                <div class="article-header-container">
                    <!-- Start CrossMark Snippet v2.0 -->
                        <!-- the external CrossMark script to load the crossmark logo responsive  JPB-5562 -->

                    <!-- End CrossMark Snippet -->                   

                    <div class="header-bar-one">
                        <h2>
                            Original Research ARTICLE

                    </h2>
                </div>
                <div class="header-bar-three">
                    Front. Plant Sci., 06 August 2019
                             | <a href="https://doi.org/10.3389/fpls.2019.01002">https://doi.org/10.3389/fpls.2019.01002</a>
                </div>
                </div>
                
<div class="JournalAbstract">
<a id="h1" name="h1"></a><h1><i>De novo</i> Assembly of the Pokeweed Genome Provides Insight Into Pokeweed Antiviral Protein (PAP) Gene Expression</h1>
<div class="authors">
<a href="https://www.frontiersin.org/people/u/780475" class="user-id-780475"><img class="pr5" src="https://loop.frontiersin.org/images/profile/780475/24" onerror="this.src='https://f96a1a95aaa960e01625-a34624e694c43cdf8b40aa048a644ca4.ssl.cf2.rackcdn.com/Design/Images/newprofile_default_profileimage_new.jpg'" alt=""/>Kira C. M. Neller</a><sup>1</sup>, <a href="https://www.frontiersin.org/people/u/634422" class="user-id-634422"><img class="pr5" src="https://loop.frontiersin.org/images/profile/634422/24" onerror="this.src='https://f96a1a95aaa960e01625-a34624e694c43cdf8b40aa048a644ca4.ssl.cf2.rackcdn.com/Design/Images/newprofile_default_profileimage_new.jpg'" alt=""/>Camille A. Diaz</a><sup>1</sup>, <a href="https://www.frontiersin.org/people/u/661911" class="user-id-661911"><img class="pr5" src="https://loop.frontiersin.org/images/profile/661911/24" onerror="this.src='https://f96a1a95aaa960e01625-a34624e694c43cdf8b40aa048a644ca4.ssl.cf2.rackcdn.com/Design/Images/newprofile_default_profileimage_new.jpg'" alt=""/>Adrian E. Platts</a><sup>2</sup> and <a href="https://www.frontiersin.org/people/u/301959" class="user-id-301959"><img class="pr5" src="https://loop.frontiersin.org/images/profile/301959/24" onerror="this.src='https://f96a1a95aaa960e01625-a34624e694c43cdf8b40aa048a644ca4.ssl.cf2.rackcdn.com/Design/Images/newprofile_default_profileimage_new.jpg'" alt=""/>Katalin A. Hudak</a><sup>1*</sup></div>
<ul class="notes">
<li><span><sup>1</sup></span>Department of Biology, York University, Toronto, ON, Canada</li>
<li><span><sup>2</sup></span>Department of Biology, Center for Genomics and Systems Biology, New York University, New York, NY, United States</li>
</ul>
<p class="mb0">Ribosome-inactivating proteins (RIPs) are RNA glycosidases thought to function in defense against pathogens. These enzymes remove purine bases from RNAs, including rRNA; the latter activity decreases protein synthesis <i>in vitro</i>, which is hypothesized to limit pathogen proliferation by causing host cell death. Pokeweed antiviral protein (PAP) is a RIP synthesized by the American pokeweed plant (<i>Phytolacca americana</i>). PAP inhibits virus infection when expressed in crop plants, yet little is known about the function of PAP in pokeweed due to a lack of genomic tools for this non-model species. In this work, we <i>de novo</i> assembled the pokeweed genome and annotated protein-coding genes. Sequencing comprised paired-end reads from a short-insert library of 83X coverage, and our draft assembly (N50 = 42.5 Kb) accounted for 74% of the measured pokeweed genome size of 1.3 Gb. We obtained 29,773 genes, 73% of which contained known protein domains, and identified several PAP isoforms. Within the gene models of each PAP isoform, a long 5&#x2032; UTR intron was discovered, which was validated by RT-PCR and sequencing. Presence of the intron stimulated reporter gene expression in tobacco. To gain further understanding of PAP regulation, we complemented this genomic resource with expression profiles of pokeweed plants subjected to stress treatments [jasmonic acid (JA), salicylic acid, polyethylene glycol, and wounding]. Cluster analysis of the top differentially expressed genes indicated that some PAP isoforms shared expression patterns with genes involved in terpenoid biosynthesis, JA-mediated signaling, and metabolism of amino acids and carbohydrates. The newly sequenced promoters of all PAP isoforms contained <i>cis</i>-regulatory elements associated with diverse biotic and abiotic stresses. These elements mediated response to JA in tobacco, based on reporter constructs containing promoter truncations of PAP-I, the most abundant isoform. Taken together, this first genomic resource for the Phytolaccaceae plant family provides new insight into the regulation and function of PAP in pokeweed.</p>
<div class="clear"></div>
</div>
<div class="JournalFullText">
<a id="h2" name="h2"></a><h2>Introduction</h2>
<p class="mb15">American pokeweed, <i>Phytolacca americana</i>, belongs to the Phytolaccaceae family of flowering plants, which comprises 65 species of herbs, shrubs, and trees. <i>P. americana</i> (pokeweed) is the most well-studied <i>Phytolacca</i> species due to its broad agricultural and medical applications. Pokeweed synthesizes PAP, an <i>N</i>-glycosidase and RIP that depurinates the conserved &#x03B1;-sarcin loop of large rRNAs (<a href="#B36">Endo et al., 1988</a>). PAP exhibits antiviral activity against diverse plant and animal viruses. Specifically, depurination of rRNA inactivates ribosomes in infected cells, thereby inhibiting host and viral protein synthesis (<a href="#B86">Lodge et al., 1993</a>; <a href="#B14">Bonness et al., 1994</a>). PAP also depurinates the genomes of some RNA viruses, interfering with multiple stages of the viral life cycle (<a href="#B50">He et al., 2008</a>; <a href="#B68">Karran and Hudak, 2008</a>; <a href="#B93">Mansouri et al., 2009</a>). Transgenic plants expressing PAP acquire novel antiviral and antifungal activities, making the gene an attractive candidate for use in agricultural engineering (<a href="#B161">Zoubenko et al., 1997</a>, <a href="#B160">2000</a>; <a href="#B144">Wang et al., 1998</a>; <a href="#B26">Dai et al., 2003</a>). Pokeweed also shows potential in phytoremediation as a heavy metal hyperaccumulator, thriving in contaminated soil that is otherwise toxic to most plants (<a href="#B110">Peng et al., 2008</a>; <a href="#B84">Liu et al., 2010</a>; <a href="#B157">Zhao et al., 2011</a>). Although this non-model plant displays resistance to diverse biotic and abiotic stresses, a wealth of information remains unknown since its genome has not been sequenced.</p>
<p class="mb15">Several isoforms of PAP have been reported, exhibiting different temporal (PAP-I, PAP-II, PAP-III) or tissue-specific (PAP-R, PAP-S, S1, S2, PAP-&#x03B1;) expression patterns, or identified during cell culture (PAP-H, PAP-C) (<a href="#B59">Irvin, 1975</a>; <a href="#B60">Irvin et al., 1980</a>; <a href="#B9">Barbieri et al., 1982</a>, <a href="#B10">1989</a>; <a href="#B13">Bolognesi et al., 1990</a>; <a href="#B69">Kataoka et al., 1992</a>; <a href="#B118">Rajamohan et al., 1999</a>; <a href="#B108">Park et al., 2002</a>). Consistent with a hypothesized role in pathogen defense, we showed previously through transcriptomic analysis that expression of several PAP isoforms is up-regulated by JA (<a href="#B102">Neller et al., 2016</a>). JA is a plant hormone that mediates resistance to insect herbivores, which are viral vectors, and necrotrophic pathogens. Others have reported an induction of RIP expression in various plants upon treatment with phytohormones [JA, salicylic acid (SA), abscisic acid (ABA)] or associated stresses, including insect feeding, pathogen infection, cold, heat, drought, salinity, and mechanical wounding (<a href="#B120">Reinbothe et al., 1994</a>; <a href="#B135">Song et al., 2000</a>; <a href="#B58">Iglesias et al., 2005</a>; <a href="#B65">Jiang et al., 2008</a>; <a href="#B116">Qin et al., 2009</a>; <a href="#B138">Tartarini et al., 2010</a>). Therefore, it is well-established that RIPs are induced by various stresses; however, it is not clear how RIP isoform expression is controlled, or how RIPs are integrated within stress-response pathways.</p>
<p class="mb15">Pokeweed is tetraploid with a chromosome count of 2n = 36 and 1C-value of 1.48 pg (&#x223C;1.5 Gb) (<a href="#B11">Bennett, 2000</a>; <a href="#B121">Rice et al., 2015</a>). Although there are no reference genomes available for the Phytolaccaceae family, genomes of some members from the order Caryophylalles (which includes pokeweed) have been sequenced. Genome assembly and annotation has been performed for <i>Beta vulgaris</i> (sugar beet), <i>Spinacia oleracea</i> (spinach), <i>Chenopodium quinoa</i> (quinoa), and <i>Amaranthus hypochondriacus</i> (amaranth) (<a href="#B29">Dohm et al., 2014</a>; <a href="#B24">Clouse et al., 2016</a>; <a href="#B62">Jarvis et al., 2017</a>; <a href="#B150">Xu et al., 2017</a>). This genomic information is a useful reference for pokeweed, especially since RIPs are prevalent among the Caryophyllales (<a href="#B28">Di Maro et al., 2014</a>).</p>
<p class="mb0">Here, we present the first <i>de novo</i> draft genome assembly of pokeweed and an annotation of its protein-coding genes. Using this resource, we investigated the presence and gene organization of PAP isoforms. A novel feature was discovered in the 5&#x2032; UTR: a long intron that affects gene expression. Integration of RNA-Seq data from pokeweed stress treatments enabled the identification of co-expressed genes and provided insight into defense responses in the plant. Finally, differences in CREs in the promoters of PAP isoforms, combined with their unique expression profiles, suggest that the isoforms have distinct roles in pokeweed. Our study provides a workflow that integrates genome assembly, annotation, and differential expression analysis for a non-model plant. These resources will facilitate the study of pokeweed to understand its ability to survive environmental stress.</p>
<a id="h3" name="h3"></a><h2>Materials and Methods</h2>
<h3 class="pt0">Genomic DNA Sequencing and Genome Assembly</h3>
<p class="mb0">A summary of our study is provided in <a href="#F1">Figure 1</a>. High-quality genomic DNA was isolated from a single pokeweed plant using a CTAB-based extraction method (<a href="#B51">Healey et al., 2014</a>) and sent to McGill University and Genome Quebec Innovation Centre (Montreal, QB, Canada) for sequencing. Shotgun sequencing was performed on one lane of an Illumina HiSeq 2500 instrument in Rapid Run mode. Paired-end reads of 250 bp were obtained from genomic DNA fragments having an average size of 400 bp; this strategy was chosen to conform with input recommendations of the downstream assembly software. Following sequencing, adapters and low-quality bases (Q &#x003C; 30, averaged over four bases) were removed with Trimmomatic (v. 0.36; <a href="#B12">Bolger et al., 2014</a>). The pokeweed genome was assembled with Discovar <i>De Novo</i> using default parameters (v. 52488; <a href="#B87">Love et al., 2016</a>) on the Sharcnet high-performance computing cluster<sup id="footnotesuper1"><a id="footnote1a"></a><a class="footnoteanchor" href="#footnote1">1</a></sup>. Raw genomic DNA sequencing reads are available at the SRA under project # PRJNA544344. The genome completion score was measured with BUSCO (v. 3; <a href="#B147">Waterhouse et al., 2018</a>).</p>
<div class="DottedLine"></div>
<div class="Imageheaders">FIGURE 1</div>
<div class="FigureDesc">
<a href="https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/465526/fpls-10-01002-HTML/image_m/fpls-10-01002-g001.jpg" name="figure1" target="_blank">
<img src="https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/465526/fpls-10-01002-HTML/image_t/fpls-10-01002-g001.gif" id="F1" alt="www.frontiersin.org" /></a>
<p><strong>Figure 1.</strong> Summary of genome assembly, annotation, and differential gene expression analysis. The assembled pokeweed genome was annotated using three sources of information provided to MAKER, indicated with dashed arrows: (i) published plant protein databases, (ii) the pokeweed <i>de novo</i> transcriptome assembly, (iii) the pokeweed genome-guided transcriptome assembly, derived from aligning unassembled (&#x2018;cleaned&#x2019;) reads to the genome (&#x2460;) and joining exonic regions into transcripts (&#x2461;). Reads from (&#x2460;) that aligned to exonic regions of a gene (defined by the annotated gene models) were summed. Counts were used as input for differential gene expression analysis. The top differentially expressed genes were clustered, and functional enrichment testing was performed on each cluster.</p>
</div>
<div class="clear"></div>
<div class="DottedLine"></div>
<h3>Determination of Genome Size</h3>
<p class="mb0">DNA contents of somatic nuclei (pg/2C) were measured using flow cytometry. For each sample, approximately 1 cm<sup>2</sup> of fresh pokeweed leaf and 2 cm<sup>2</sup> of fresh <i>Sorghum bicolor</i> Pioneer 8695 (1.74 pg/2C; <a href="#B66">Johnston et al., 1999</a>) were chopped with a razor blade in 0.7 mL of ice-cold LB01 buffer (<a href="#B30">Dolezel et al., 1989</a>) containing 100 &#x03BC;g/mL propidium iodide and 50 &#x03BC;g/mL RNAse A. Samples were stained at room temperature for 20&#x2013;25 min, then tested at low speed using a BD FACSCalibur flow cytometer (BD Biosciences, San Jos&#x00E9;, CA, United States). The FL2 detector (585/42 nm) was used to measure fluorescence, with integrated fluorescence as the parameter of interest. 2C DNA contents were calculated based on the relative fluorescence of the pokeweed and <i>Sorghum</i> G0/G1 nuclei and the known DNA content of <i>Sorghum</i>. Means, coefficients of variation, and nuclei numbers for the nuclei fluorescence peaks were measured using flowPloidy (v. 1.7.0; <a href="#B133">Smith et al., 2018</a>). Nine pokeweed plants were tested in total: four as individuals and five as bulk samples of two or three plants.</p>
<h3>Stress Treatments, mRNA Sequencing, and Transcriptome Assembly</h3>
<p class="mb15">Total RNA was extracted from four-leaf pokeweed plants subjected to the following treatments: sprayed with 5 mM JA or SA (solubilized in 0.5% ET), watered every 3 days for a 7-day period with 10% PEG, or wounded with forceps (WND). Plants sprayed with 0.5% ET or WT served as controls for JA/SA and PEG/WND, respectively. Leaf tissue was flash-frozen in liquid nitrogen 24 h following treatment for JA, SA, ET, and WND samples, and 3 days after the final treatment for PEG and WT samples. RNA-Seq libraries were constructed with the TruSeq Stranded mRNA Library Preparation Kit (RS-122-2101, Illumina). For each condition, four biological replicate libraries were prepared from equal amounts of RNA pooled from three independent plants (i.e., 24 libraries derived from 72 total plants). Strand-specific, paired-end reads of 125 bp were sequenced on two lanes of an Illumina HiSeq 2500 instrument by The Centre for Applied Genomics (The Hospital for Sick Children, Toronto, ON, Canada). Raw mRNA sequencing reads are available at the SRA under project # PRJNA309999.</p>
<p class="mb0">In addition to the data generated in the present study, mRNA transcriptome assembly incorporated two publicly available <i>P. americana</i> RNA-Seq datasets from the SRA: Accessions SRX2774676 and ERX2099309, which were derived from whole plants (root, stem, leaf, and flower tissue), as well as high-coverage RNA-Seq datasets (<i>n</i> = 3 biological replicates) from our previous study of the pokeweed leaf mRNA transcriptome (<a href="#B102">Neller et al., 2016</a>). All datasets (32 in total) were processed with Trimmomatic as described above. Two transcriptome assembly strategies were employed. For <i>de novo</i> assembly, reads were combined into a single reference transcriptome using Trinity (v. 2.5.1; <a href="#B44">Grabherr et al., 2011</a>; <a href="#B47">Haas et al., 2013</a>). For genome-guided assembly, RNA-Seq libraries were independently aligned to the assembled genome with HISAT (v. 2.1.0; <a href="#B70">Kim et al., 2015</a>) and transcript reconstruction was performed with Stringtie (v. 1.3.4b; <a href="#B111">Pertea et al., 2015</a>). The independent assemblies were combined into a single, non-redundant reference transcriptome using the Stringtie &#x2018;merge&#x2019; function.</p>
<h3>Genome Annotation</h3>
<p class="mb15">Only contigs of minimum length 10 Kb were annotated, as these were most likely to contain full-length protein-coding genes (<a href="#B18">Campbell et al., 2014a</a>). Prior to annotation, a pokeweed-specific repeat library was prepared with RepeatModeler (v. 1.0.9)<sup id="footnotesuper2"><a id="footnote2a"></a><a class="footnoteanchor" href="#footnote2">2</a></sup>. Repeat-masking and gene prediction were performed using the MAKER pipeline (v. 2.31.8; <a href="#B18">Campbell et al., 2014a</a>,<a href="#B19">b</a>). Both pokeweed-specific and simple repeats were masked with RepeatMasker (v. 4.0.7)<sup id="footnotesuper3"><a id="footnote3a"></a><a class="footnoteanchor" href="#footnote3">3</a></sup>. RepeatRunner was used to mask divergent protein-coding portions of retro-elements and retro-viruses not identified by RepeatMasker (<a href="#B132">Smith et al., 2007</a>).</p>
<p class="mb15">Gene prediction followed the protocol outlined in <a href="#B18">Campbell et al. (2014a)</a>. The Trinity (<i>de novo</i>) and Stringtie (genome-guided) transcriptomes described above were provided as transcript evidence. The proteomes of published Caryophyllales species (sugar beet, spinach, quinoa, amaranth) were used as homologous protein evidence (<a href="#B29">Dohm et al., 2014</a>; <a href="#B24">Clouse et al., 2016</a>; <a href="#B62">Jarvis et al., 2017</a>; <a href="#B150">Xu et al., 2017</a>). The quinoa and amaranth proteomes were obtained from Phytozome<sup id="footnotesuper4"><a id="footnote4a"></a><a class="footnoteanchor" href="#footnote4">4</a></sup> (v. 1.0 and v. 2.1, respectively), spinach from SpinachBase<sup id="footnotesuper5"><a id="footnote5a"></a><a class="footnoteanchor" href="#footnote5">5</a></sup> (v. 1), and sugar beet from The <i>Beta vulgaris</i> Resource (Refbeet-1.1)<sup id="footnotesuper6"><a id="footnote6a"></a><a class="footnoteanchor" href="#footnote6">6</a></sup>. Protein evidence was further supplemented with the plant subset of the SwissProt database (obtained October 2017; <a href="#B114">Pundir et al., 2017</a>). Three rounds of iterative gene prediction were performed with MAKER. In Round 1, predictions were inferred directly from transcript and protein evidence (est2genome, protein2genome = 1). Top-scoring gene models from Round 1 (AED &#x2264; 0.25, amino acids &#x2265; 50) were used to train the <i>ab initio</i> gene predictors SNAP (<a href="#B75">Korf, 2004</a>) and AUGUSTUS (v. 3.2.3; <a href="#B136">Stanke et al., 2004</a>). In Round 2, MAKER was re-run with <i>ab initio</i> gene predictors turned on, and top-scoring models were used to re-train predictors as per above. A final Round 3 was run with the twice-trained gene predictors.</p>
<p class="mb0">Following gene prediction, an Interproscan (v. 5.28-67.0; <a href="#B155">Zdobnov and Apweiler, 2001</a>) search was performed to identify protein-coding (Pfam) domains and obtain associated GO terms. GO annotations were supplemented with non-redundant GO terms from the SwissProt database for the best hit as per BLAST-P analysis. Each round of genome annotation was inspected visually with JBrowse (v. 1.12.3 <a href="#B17">Buels et al., 2016</a>). In Rounds 2 and 3, we observed spurious fusion of protein-coding gene models with putative pseudogenes; this could not be resolved despite several attempts of re-training and post-annotation processing. Therefore, the final gene set was conservatively chosen to comprise only the evidence-based annotations from Round 1. The completion score of the annotated gene set was measured with BUSCO as described above, as well as relative to CoreGFs of the &#x2018;green plants&#x2019; subset from PLAZA v. 2.5 (<a href="#B141">Veeckman et al., 2016</a>).</p>
<h3>Validation of PAP Isoform Gene Models</h3>
<p class="mb0">cDNAs were generated by reverse transcribing 0.5 &#x03BC;g of total pokeweed RNA with SuperScript III reverse transcriptase (25 units; Thermo Fisher) and isoform-specific primers. To validate PAP isoform gene models at both the genomic and mRNA levels, PCR was performed with isoform-specific forward and reverse primers using either pokeweed gDNA or cDNA as the starting template. All PCR amplifications were conducted using Q5 High-Fidelity DNA polymerase (1 unit; New England Biolabs) and 0.5 &#x03BC;M of each primer. PCR products were gel-purified and used as templates for a second round of PCR, this time with forward and reverse primers that contained additional sequences for cloning. Amplicons from the second round of PCR were gel-purified and cloned into the multiple cloning site of pHSG299 vector using the one-step SLIC (sequence- and ligation-independent cloning) method (<a href="#B64">Jeong et al., 2012</a>). All constructs were sequenced and compared with computationally derived gene and mRNA models. Primers used in this study are listed in <a href="#S9">Supplementary Table 1</a>.</p>
<h3>Orthogroup Analysis</h3>
<p class="mb0">The longest isoform per gene was obtained for each Caryophyllales proteome noted above, and orthogroup assignment was performed with OrthoFinder (v. 2.2.6; <a href="#B35">Emms and Kelly, 2015</a>). Functional enrichment analysis of genes from pokeweed-specific orthogroups was conducted with the GOseq package (<a href="#B153">Young et al., 2010</a>) in the statistical program R (<a href="#B117">R Foundation for Statistical Computing, 2016</a>). Pokeweed-specific genes were tested for enriched GO terms relative to all pokeweed genes.</p>
<h3>Differential Gene Expression Analysis</h3>
<p class="mb0">HTSeq (v. 0.8.0; <a href="#B5">Anders et al., 2015</a>) was used to sum exon-level counts per gene for each independent RNA-Seq library aligned to the pokeweed genome. Gene counts were provided as input for differential expression testing in the Bioconductor package EdgeR (v. 3.7; <a href="#B123">Robinson et al., 2010</a>). All pairwise tests were performed with four biological replicates per treatment, and genes with FDR &#x003C; 0.05 in at least one comparison were considered differentially expressed. Treatment-responsive genes were identified based on the relevant tests: ET vs. JA or SA, and WT vs. PEG or WND. Top DEGs were defined as those with FDR &#x003C; 0.001 and FC &#x003E; 4 in at least one pairwise comparison. These genes were clustered with DEclust (v. 1.0.1; <a href="#B6">Aoto et al., 2017</a>) based on a multi-conditional expression profile of EdgeR results and normalized abundance in TPM. GO enrichment analysis was performed on the genes in each cluster as described above.</p>
<h3>Quantitative RT-PCR (qRT-PCR) Validation of PAP Isoform mRNA Levels</h3>
<p class="mb0">cDNAs were generated by reverse transcribing 0.5 &#x03BC;g of total pokeweed RNA with SuperScript III reverse transcriptase (25 units; Thermo Fisher) and gene-specific primers (<a href="#S9">Supplementary Table 1</a>) according to the manufacturer&#x2019;s instructions. Elongation factor-1-gamma (EF1G) and the cell wall protein BIIDXI served as internal controls as these transcripts were stably expressed under our stress treatments according to our RNA-Seq differential expression analysis. Following cDNA synthesis, 5 &#x03BC;L of the reverse transcription (RT) reaction product was combined with 0.3 &#x03BC;M forward primer, 0.3 &#x03BC;M reverse primer, and 33 &#x03BC;L of 2X SYBR Green qPCR Master Mix (Bimake), to a final volume of 66 &#x03BC;L. Each reaction was split into three technical replicates. qRT-PCRs were conducted in a QIAGEN RotorGene Q thermocycler with the following settings: hold at 50&#x00B0;C for 20 s, initial denaturation and hot-start DNA polymerase activation for 10 min, followed by 40 cycles alternating between denaturation (95&#x00B0;C; 15 s) and combined annealing/extension (68&#x00B0;C, 45 s). mRNA levels were quantified using the &#x0394;&#x0394;Ct method (<a href="#B85">Livak and Schmittgen, 2001</a>). A melting curve analysis was performed to confirm the presence of a single PCR product after each reaction. At least three biological replicates per treatment were conducted for each transcript.</p>
<h3>Generation of Promoter-GUS Reporter Constructs</h3>
<p class="mb15">The 1262 bp region upstream of the PAP-I TSS was considered the proximal PAP-I promoter. This &#x223C;1.3 Kb promoter, along with the 5&#x2032; UTR and 1.6 Kb intron, were PCR-amplified from pokeweed genomic DNA (500 ng) with the primers PAP-I-prom-SLIC-FOR and PAP-I-prom-SLIC-REV (0.5 &#x03BC;M each). Amplicons were gel-purified and cloned by one-step SLIC (<a href="#B64">Jeong et al., 2012</a>) into the multiple cloning site of pHSG299. All PCR amplifications were conducted using Q5 High-Fidelity DNA polymerase (1 unit; New England Biolabs) using the manufacturer&#x2019;s instructions. The pHSG299 plasmid containing the PAP-I promoter and intron then served as the PCR template for all downstream PCRs of PAP-I promoter fragments. PAP-I promoter fragments containing the 1.6 Kb intron (1262-int and 102-int) were generated through PCR by pairing the same reverse primer (5-UTR-SLIC-REV) with different forward primers (P1-FOR or P7-FOR). Primer sequences are listed in <a href="#S9">Supplementary Table 1</a>.</p>
<p class="mb15">To produce intronless versions of promoter constructs, a reverse primer (5-UTR-no-int-REV) was designed to connect the two portions of the PAP-I 5&#x2032; UTR that were originally interrupted by the intron. This reverse primer was paired with different forward primers (P1-FOR to P7-FOR) to produce 5&#x2032; promoter truncations (1262, 1124, 711, 584, 432, 296, and 102). PCR products were gel-purified and used as templates for a second round of PCR to attach sequences needed for SLIC cloning.</p>
<p class="mb15">Amplified fragments were cloned in place of the CaMV 35S promoter in pCambia 0305.2 using one-step SLIC (<a href="#B64">Jeong et al., 2012</a>) and transformed into <i>Escherichia coli</i> DH5&#x03B1;. For constructs used to investigate the effect of the PAP-I leader intron on gene expression, the castor bean catalase intron in the GUS reporter gene was removed to determine the influence of the PAP-I intron alone. Minus catalase intron constructs were made by excluding the intron through PCR (Primer pairs: pCambia-1-no-cat-FOR and pCambia-1-no-cat-REV; pCambia-2-no-cat-FOR and pCambia-2-no-cat-REV) and reassembling the two fragments through Gibson assembly (Cat# E2611S; New England Biolabs). Positive <i>E. coli</i> transformants were screened through colony PCR, and all reporter gene constructs were confirmed by sequencing.</p>
<p class="mb0">Constructs were transformed into <i>Rhizobium radiobacter</i> (syn. Agrobacterium) AGL1 strain by electroporation as previously described (<a href="#B149">Wise et al., 2006</a>). Electrocompetent cells (20 &#x03BC;L) mixed with plasmid DNA (50 ng) were electroporated (2.5 kV, 25 &#x03BC;F capacitance, and 400 &#x03A9; resistance) and allowed to recover in 2 mL of non-selective YEP medium for 2 h. After recovery, 100 &#x03BC;L of cells were plated on selective YEP agar (50 &#x03BC;g/mL carbenicillin, 50 &#x03BC;g/mL kanamycin) and incubated at 28&#x00B0;C to allow colonies to form.</p>
<h3>Measurement of Reporter Activity (GUS Histochemical and Fluorometric Assay)</h3>
<p class="mb15">Agrobacterium cultures harboring promoter-reporter gene constructs were used to agroinfiltrate leaves of four-leaf stage <i>Nicotiana tabacum</i> plants as previously described (<a href="#B156">Zhao et al., 2017</a>). Cultures were grown in selective YEP liquid medium (50 &#x03BC;g/mL carbenicillin, 50 &#x03BC;g/mL kanamycin) until late log phase (OD<sub>600</sub> = 0.7 &#x2013; 1.0). Cells were then pelleted by centrifugation, washed in agroinfiltration solution (10 mM MES-KOH, pH 5.6, 10 mM MgCl2, 200 &#x03BC;M acetosyringone), and resuspended in agroinfiltration solution to a final OD<sub>600</sub> of 0.5. Agrobacterium cells were injected into the abaxial surface of leaves using a needleless syringe. For the JA experiment, leaves were treated with either 0 mM JA (0.5% ET; mock) or 5 mM JA 24 h after agroinfiltration. Leaf disks from inoculated plants were harvested 72 h post agroinfiltration and either used directly for histochemical assays or stored in liquid nitrogen until processing for the fluorometric assay.</p>
<p class="mb15">GUS histochemical assays were performed according to <a href="#B63">Jefferson et al. (1987)</a> with some modifications. Fresh leaf disks (0.5 cm diameter) from inoculated plants (minimum of three plants per construct) were vacuum-infiltrated with 5-bromo-4-chloro-3-indolyl-<i>b</i>-<small>D</small>-glucuronic acid, cyclohexylammonium salt (X-Gluc) solution, and incubated overnight at 37&#x00B0;C. After GUS staining, leaf disks were cleared of chlorophyll by washing in increasing concentrations of ET (70&#x2013;100%) for 48 h.</p>
<p class="mb0">GUS fluorometric assays were performed in black, clear-bottom 96-well plates according to <a href="#B25">C&#x00F4;t&#x00E9; and Rutledge (2003)</a>, with some modifications. Frozen leaf disks (1 cm diameter; 2 disks per sample) from inoculated plants (minimum of four plants per construct) were combined with 200 mg of glass beads (1.0 mm, BioSpec) and 300 &#x03BC;L of GUS extraction buffer (50 mM phosphate buffer, pH 7.0, 10 mM DTT, 10 mM EDTA, 0.1% SDS, 0.1% Triton X-100, 10 mM &#x03B2;-mercaptoethanol), and homogenized using a 3110BX MiniBeadBeater (BioSpec) for 120 s at speed 48. Tissue debris was removed by centrifugation at 4&#x00B0;C and cleared plant extracts (10 &#x03BC;L) were mixed with 720 &#x03BC;L of 0.1 mM 4-methylumbelliferyl-&#x03B2;-<small>D</small>-glucuronide hydrate (4-MUG) and incubated at 37&#x00B0;C. Beginning at 0 min, a 10-&#x03BC;L aliquot was taken from each reaction every 15 min for a total of 60 min, and pipetted into a well containing 180 &#x03BC;L of stop buffer (0.2 M Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>). Three technical replicates per time point were taken for each sample. Fluorescence values were measured at room temperature using a Synergy H4 Hybrid microplate reader (excitation: 365 nm; emission: 455 nm) and compared to a previously determined 4-methylumbelliferone (4-MU) standard curve. GUS activity was calculated from the linear slope of the fluorescence readings and normalized to the total protein concentration, which was determined using a BCA Reducing Agent Compatible Protein Assay Kit (G-Biosciences). Comparisons between mock-treated and JA-treated samples (<i>p</i> &#x003C; 0.01) were performed for each promoter construct using two-tailed <i>t</i>-tests.</p>
<h3>Identification of Putative PAP Promoter CREs and JA-Responsive Transcription Factors</h3>
<p class="mb0">As with PAP-I, the 1.3 Kb sequence upstream of the TSS was considered the proximal promoter for each PAP isoform. Promoter sequence identity analysis was performed using Clustal Omega<sup id="footnotesuper7"><a id="footnote7a"></a><a class="footnoteanchor" href="#footnote7">7</a></sup> with default parameters. Putative plant-specific CREs were identified using PLACE<sup id="footnotesuper8"><a id="footnote8a"></a><a class="footnoteanchor" href="#footnote8">8</a></sup> and PlantPan 2.0<sup id="footnotesuper9"><a id="footnote9a"></a><a class="footnoteanchor" href="#footnote9">9</a></sup> web interfaces (<a href="#B53">Higo et al., 1999</a>; <a href="#B23">Chow et al., 2016</a>). To reduce false positives, only sequences with &#x2265;90% identity to the published motifs were included. Putative TFs associated with CREs were identified in the pokeweed genome based on annotated Pfam domains, and their differential expression results were assessed for JA-responsiveness (FDR &#x003C; 0.05).</p>
<a id="h4" name="h4"></a><h2>Results</h2>
<h3 class="pt0">Assessment of the Pokeweed Genome Assembly</h3>
<p class="mb0">Statistics of the pokeweed genome assembly are shown in <a href="#T1">Table 1</a>. Based on 1 Kb+ contigs, the assembly is 0.93 Gb in size and has a read coverage of 83X. The contig and scaffold N50 values are 35.2 and 42.5 Kb, respectively, where a scaffold represents the single highest coverage path through each line of the genome assembly graph. The scaffold assembly was used for all downstream analysis. The assembly had a BUSCO genome completion score of 84.3%; of a possible 1440 plant BUSCOs, 1214 were identified as complete (1142 single-copy and 72 duplicated), 82 were fragmented, and 144 were missing. For an additional metric of assembly completion, we determined the expected size of the pokeweed genome using flow cytometry (<a href="#S9">Supplementary Figure 1</a>). Based on triplicate samples of four individual plants and two non-replicate bulk samples, the pokeweed plants used in this study had a genomic content (mean &#x00B1; SEM) of 2.57 pg/2C &#x00B1; 0.0019. Variability was low, with all 14 samples measuring either 2.57 or 2.58 pg/2C. Pokeweed and <i>Sorghum</i> nuclei fluorescence peaks had coefficients of variation &#x003C;3.3% and included data from at least 1,000 nuclei. Given the conversion that 1 pg DNA = 980 Mb, the haploid genome size of pokeweed is estimated to be 1.26 Gb. Therefore, the present assembly represents 74% of the expected genome size.</p>
